    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="6132eac8306a4893ac990fce7013ee43">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '6e9cb72d-2188-411f-bbb2-9c1d5d0381a4'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d73763132" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Hollow core–shell Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>Ni<sub>0.6</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> with tiny-void space was synthesized by one-step spray drying and calcination for the first time due to the different viscosity, coordination ability, ratios, and shrinkage rates between CA and PVP, which ensures high energy density and relieves volume expansion, combining with regulation on Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path and energy barrier by doping Ni to achieve fast charging and low-temperature sodium storage.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/da9bdb7c-94bd-41d1-a9f7-501a2fa63bb0/anie202410590-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/da9bdb7c-94bd-41d1-a9f7-501a2fa63bb0/anie202410590-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7cebff01-aea0-415e-9716-13a752b9e95b/anie202410590-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/da9bdb7c-94bd-41d1-a9f7-501a2fa63bb0/anie202410590-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d73763152" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Iron-based mixed polyanion phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP) is recognized as a promising cathode for Sodium-ion Batteries (SIBs) due to its low cost and environmental friendliness. However, its inherent low conductivity and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion limit fast charge and low-temperature sodium storage. This study pioneers a scalable synthesis of hollow core–shelled Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>Ni<sub>0.6</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> with tiny-void space (THoCS-0.6Ni) via a one-step spray-drying combined with calcination process due to the different viscosity, coordination ability, molar ratios, and shrinkage rates between citric acid and polyvinylpyrrolidone. This unique structure with interconnected carbon networks ensures rapid electron transport and fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, as well as efficient space utilization for relieving volume expansion. Incorporating regulation of lattice structure by doping Ni heteroatom to effectively improve intrinsic electron conductivity and optimize Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path and energy barrier, which achieves fast charge and low-temperature sodium storage. As a result, THoCS-0.6Ni exhibits superior rate capability (86.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 25 C). Notably, THoCS-0.6Ni demonstrates exceptional cycling stability at −20 °C with a capacity of 43.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2500 cycles at 5 C. This work provides a universal strategy to design the hollow core–shelled structure with tiny-void space cathode materials for reversible batteries with fast-charge and low-temperature Na-storage features.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410590-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410590-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion Batteries (SIBs) exhibit extensive potential as large-scale energy storage owing to the advantages of abundant resources, safety and low cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0001_R_d73763123e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small">1</a>-<a href="#anie202410590-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0003_R_d73763123e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span> Wherein, the cathode as a key configuration has an enormous influence on electrochemical performance. Many cathode materials have been developed in the last few decades, including prussian blue analogues,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0004_R_d73763123e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span> layered transition metal oxides,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0005_R_d73763123e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - InfoMat">5</a></sup></span> polyanion compounds,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0006_R_d73763123e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Res.">6</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0007_R_d73763123e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Green Energy &amp; Environ.">7</a></sup></span> as well as sodium-bonding organic compounds.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0008_R_d73763123e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span> Among them, the iron-based polyanionic mixed phosphate of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP) has attracted significant attention in recent years due to its high theoretical capacity (129 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), suitable voltage plateau (higher than 3.1 V), and prominent low cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0009_R_d73763123e765" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">9</a></sup></span> However, the low inherent electronic conductivity and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of NFPP largely limit the development of the fast-charge and low-temperature reversible Na-storage features.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0010_R_d73763123e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0011_R_d73763123e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Lattice regulation by doping heterogeneous elements is an effective method to adjust ion transport channels and electron distribution for improving intrinsic ion diffusion and electronic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0012_R_d73763123e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Mater. Today">12</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0013_R_d73763123e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a></sup></span> Moreover, the lattice regulation induces a decrease in the Na<sup>+</sup> transport energy barrier, which is beneficial to improve the reaction kinetics and reduce the lattice stress during cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0014_R_d73763123e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0015_R_d73763123e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span> Therefore, doping elements such as Mg,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0016_R_d73763123e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span> Mn,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0017_R_d73763123e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a></sup></span> at the Fe site of NFPP have been reported to facilitate the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> and electron transport rate, which provides the possibility for fast-charging and low-temperature Na storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0018_R_d73763123e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a></sup></span> However, inactive elemental doping tends to sacrifice capacity, there are still rooms for improvement in the area of low-temperature fast-charging and high-rate long-cycle performance, and there is still not a clear understanding of the relationship between electrochemical behavior of NFPP and doping concentration.</p>
         
         <p>Hollow structure design by embedding NFPP particles into carbon matrix is another strategy to improve fast-charge and low-temperature electrochemical performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0019_R_d73763123e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span> The unique structure can facilitate the permeation of electrolyte to increase the contact area with electrodes, thus providing more activity for redox reaction and shortening the diffusion path of Na<sup>+</sup>, which improves Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0020_R_d73763123e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci.">20</a>-<a href="#anie202410590-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0022_R_d73763123e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></sup></span> In addition, the highly conductive three-dimensional carbon network structure fosters fast electron transfer between NFPP particles and disperses the structural stress during charging and discharging.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0023_R_d73763123e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a>-<a href="#anie202410590-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0025_R_d73763123e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a></sup></span> Therefore, combining with heteroatom doping to synthesize hollow structural materials could promote the fast transfer of electrons and ions. However, the existing synthesis methods for hollow structural heteroatom-doped NFPP/C are tedious and complex. Meanwhile, the free space inner shell realizes a lower volume utilization to influence the energy density per unit weight or volume, which is very important for large-scale energy storage applications. Therefore, it is desirable to construct simply hierarchical hollow structural heteroatom doped NFPP/C with tiny space to just relieve the weeny volume expansion and achieve fast electron and ion transfer rate for fast-charging and low-temperature Na storage.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we pioneer a scalable strategy for hollow core–shelled Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3-x</sub>Ni<sub>x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> with tiny-void space (THoCS-xNi, <i>x</i>=0, 0.3, 0.6, 0.9) via a one-step spray-drying combined with calcination process. Wherein, citric acid (CA) chelating with Fe<sup>3+</sup> forms NFPP/C core, polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) forms N-doped carbon shell creatively, due to the different viscosity during spray-drying process, coordination ability, and shrinkage rates during calcination between citric acid and PVP. This unique structure with interconnected carbon networks ensures rapid electron transport and fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, as well as efficient space utilization for just relieve volume expansion. Incorporating regulation of lattice structure by doping Ni heteroatom to effectively improve intrinsic electron conductivity and optimize Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path and energy barrier, which achieves fast charge and low-temperature Na storage.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410590-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410590-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The formation mechanism of THoCS-xNi is detected in detail. As we know, CA has three carboxyl groups and one hydroxyl group, which can chelate with metal ions such as Fe<sup>3+</sup> in NFPP to form a strong multi-point coordination compound with lower viscosity by providing electrons to occupy the empty d orbital of Fe<sup>3+</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0001">1a</a>). However, as a molecular polymer with higher viscosity, the PVP has a relatively indirect interaction with metal ions. During the spray drying process, the multi-point coordination compound with lower viscosity is preferred to aggregate to form an inner core, and the PVP with higher viscosity tends to diffuse to the outer and wrap the inner core. After that, during the adjacent calcination process, since the difference in decomposition rates between citric acid and PVP (Figure S1), the inner multi-point coordination compound core gradually shrinks and separates from the outer carbon shell originated from PVP with the increase of temperature, forming the THoCS-xNi at 550 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0001">1b</a>). This interesting phenomenon was further testified by the TEM results of products at different calcination temperatures (Figure S2). Moreover, the SEM and TEM images of samples with different CA to PVP ratios further demonstrated the formation mechanism of hollow core–shelled structures with tiny-void space (Figure S3 and S4).This unique hollow core–shelled structure with tiny-void space can jointly alleviate the weeny volume change to maintain the high volume energy density and shorten Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths, largely surpassing the solid sphere (the highest volume energy density, but the highest volume expansion and lowest sodium diffusion coefficient) and hollow sphere (the lowest volume expansion and highest sodium diffusion coefficient, but lowest volume energy density) as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0001">1c</a>. Moreover, the Ni-doping, substituting the Fe in NFPP, facilitates to reducing the band gap of NFPP, enhancing its intrinsic electronic conductivity. As the cation radius of Ni<sup>2+</sup> is larger than that of Fe<sup>2+</sup> in NFPP, the Ni-doping leads to the expansion of the lattice structure and lowers the energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion with varying doping ratios (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0001">1d</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410590-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/47b8e950-8613-48fe-ae83-faaac5e4c70b/anie202410590-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/47b8e950-8613-48fe-ae83-faaac5e4c70b/anie202410590-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0c198807-d0b8-47a5-ae1a-834b413aa4d4/anie202410590-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/47b8e950-8613-48fe-ae83-faaac5e4c70b/anie202410590-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410590-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410590" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Schematic diagram of the synthesis process of the THoCS-xNi sample; (b) The formation mechanism of THoCS-xNi; (c) The advantages of different structures; (d) The comparison of THoCS and the THoCS-0.6Ni samples. (e) High magnification TEM images of THoCS-0.6Ni; (f) EDS mappings.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The morphology of THoCS-0.6Ni was first studied by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). As shown in Figure S5, the THoCS-0.6Ni displays a regular spherical shape with a diameter ranging from 1 to 8 μm for a high tap density of 1.33 g cm<sup>−3</sup> and the morphology is minimally affected by doping varying amounts of Ni. By comparing the tap density with the samples at different calcination temperatures and CA/PVP ratios, the optimal sample THoCS-0.6Ni has a significant advantage (Figure S6 and S7), even compared with other cathode materials (Table S1). The TEM image (Figure S8a) demonstrates a solid core inside of the carbon shell, and a thin interconnected carbon network is also observed for high electronic conductivity and structural stability. In the magnified images (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0001">1e</a>), a distinct core–shelled sphere structure with a space between the thin outer carbon layer and the solid core is prominently observed. Furthermore, the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image in Figure S8b reveals distinct parallel stripes with a lattice spacing of 0.52 nm, corresponding to the (002) crystal plane of NFPP. The energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS mapping) image also revealed a homogeneous distribution of Na, Fe, Ni, C, N, P, and O elements throughout the selected region without any other detectable elements, thereby signifying the high purity of THoCS-0.6Ni sample (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0001">1f</a>). In addition, the area occupied by C and N elements is similar but is significantly larger than that of Fe, implying the well encapsulation of the carbon shell. As there is no N in citric acid, the formation of the outermost carbon shell is derived from the pyrolysis of PVP.</p>
         
         <p>The crystal structures of THoCS-xNi samples were characterized using X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0002">2a</a>, all diffraction peaks highly agree with the previously reported NFPP diffraction peaks, confirming their classification within the orthorhombic and <i>Pn2<sub>1</sub>a</i> space group.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0026_R_d73763123e915" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a></sup></span> With the increase of doping Ni content at the Fe site, the diffraction peak at 2θ=33.67°, referring to the (222) crystal plane, is slightly left-shift, indicating an inducing expansion of lattice structure of THoCS due to a larger radius of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (0.069 nm) substituting to Fe<sup>2+</sup>(0.061 nm).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0027_R_d73763123e923" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a></sup></span> Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0002">2b</a> shows the crystal structure of THoCS-0.6Ni. The crystal structure of THoCS-0.6Ni is composed of two PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedrons and three Fe(Ni)O<sub>6</sub> octahedrons, which are parallel to the b-axis and c-axis of [Fe(Ni)<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>]. The infinite layer of [Fe<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>]<sub>∞</sub> along the a-direction is connected by [P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>] groups, thus forming a large main tunnel along the b-direction.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0028_R_d73763123e953" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nat. Commun.">28</a></sup></span> In addition, the Rietveld refinement results show that the cell volume increases after Ni doping (Figure S9 and Table S2). The different interactions with molecular bonds and functional groups of THoCS-xNi samples were investigated using Fourier transform infrared spectra (FT-IR) and Raman spectra. In the FT-IR spectra, the peaks in the ranges of 400 cm<sup>−1</sup> to 700 cm<sup>−1</sup>, and 975 cm<sup>−1</sup> to 1300 cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to O−P-O stretching and P−O bending vibrations within the PO<sub>4</sub> group (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0002">2c</a>),<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0029_R_d73763123e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Alloys Compd.">29</a></sup></span> and the peaks within the range of 721 cm<sup>−1</sup> to 956 cm<sup>−1</sup> are ascribed to the symmetric and antisymmetric vibrations of P−O−P in the P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> group,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0030_R_d73763123e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Commun.">30</a></sup></span> originated from THoCS. In Raman spectra (Figure S10), the prominent peak observed at 1012.5 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds primarily to the stretching vibration of the FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedron,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0028_R_d73763123e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nat. Commun.">28</a></sup></span> verifying the phase of THoCS combined with the above results of FT-IR. Besides, the results indicate that there are abundant defects in the carbon layer after doping Ni which is beneficial to promote the rapid diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0031_R_d73763123e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">31</a></sup></span> The carbon content for THoCS, THoCS-0.3Ni, THoCS-0.6Ni, and THoCS-0.9Ni are analyzed to be 6.26, 5.97, 5.53, and 5.70 wt % by thermogravimetric (TG) analysis (Figure S11), respectively. The X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (XPS) test was conducted to determine the element electronic information. The full survey spectra of THoCS-xNi (Figure S12) effectively captures the distinctive signals originating from Na 1s, Fe 2p, Ni 2p, C 1s, N 1s, P 2p, and O 1s. The high-resolution C 1s XPS spectra (Figure S13a) is divided into four peaks at 284.6, 285.1, 286.3, and 289.3 eV, corresponding to the sp<sup>2</sup> C, sp<sup>3</sup> C, C−N, and O-C=O bonds.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0032_R_d73763123e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Eng. Sci.">32</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0033_R_d73763123e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span> And peaks corresponding to three different nitrogen states (Figure S13b), named pyridinic N at 398.6 eV, pyrrolic N at 400.8 eV, and quaternary N at 403.1 eV were detected in the high-resolution N 1s XPS spectra.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0034_R_d73763123e1005" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Environ. Mater.">34</a></sup></span> The above results prove that the N element is doped into carbon layer, thereby facilitating enhanced electron transport.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0035_R_d73763123e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Power Sources">35</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0036_R_d73763123e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">36</a></sup></span> Additionally, the high-resolution spectra of Fe 2p in THoCS-0.6Ni shows the existence of Fe in the +2 oxidation state (Figure S13c).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0037_R_d73763123e1012" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">37</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0038_R_d73763123e1012" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ChemSusChem">38</a></sup></span> The local coordination environment and chemical state of Fe in THoCS-0.6Ni were further investigated through the Fe K-edge XANES spectra, of which references are Fe foil, FeO, and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> with metallic Fe<sup>0</sup>, Fe<sup>2+</sup>, and Fe<sup>3+</sup>, respectively. The results demonstrate that the pre-edge curves of THoCS and THoCS-0.6Ni are close to FeO (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0002">2d</a>), suggesting a valence state of +2 for Fe.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0039_R_d73763123e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a></sup></span> In addition, the absorption edge of THoCS-0.6Ni is slightly shifted towards higher energies, indicating a slightly increased oxidation state of iron. The coordination environments of Fe in THoCS and THoCS-0.6Ni were investigated via the Fourier-transformed extended EXAFS in R-space (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0002">2e</a>). After Ni doping, the Fe−O bond length increases from 1.56 Å to 1.59 Å due to the lattice expansion, and the longer Fe−O bond corresponds to a more open THoCS crystal lattice, which produces a more easily transportable active site and thus improves the activity of the reaction. In addition, we performed a wavelet-transform (WT) Fe K-edge EXAFS oscillation analysis to further analyze the coordination environment of Fe in THoCS-0.6Ni.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0040_R_d73763123e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">40</a></sup></span> The results show that THoCS-0.6Ni exhibits the maximum intensity at 5.2 Å<sup>−1</sup>, which is indexed to Fe−O bond (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0002">2f</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410590-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6c92a988-b1c6-4bc8-a517-ce4c090a25d2/anie202410590-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6c92a988-b1c6-4bc8-a517-ce4c090a25d2/anie202410590-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2850ff5a-947a-48d2-a23b-3d5cf58f3daa/anie202410590-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6c92a988-b1c6-4bc8-a517-ce4c090a25d2/anie202410590-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410590-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410590" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) XRD patterns of THoCS-xNi samples; (b) The schematic illustration of THoCS and THoCS-0.6Ni crystal structure; (c) FTIR spectra of THoCS-xNi samples; (d) XANES, (e) FT-EXAFS spectra, and (f) the WT of THoCS-0.6Ni with Fe foil, FeO, and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> as reference samples at the Fe K-edge.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In order to investigate the effect of doping ratio on electrochemical performance, half-cells using THoCS-xNi as cathode and Na foil as anode were assembled, of which electrochemical behavior was checked by the cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge–discharge test. Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0003">3a</a> displays the CV curves at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> within a potential range of 1.5–4.2 V. Two pairs of major redox peaks corresponding to reversible Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> reactions are detected in all samples.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0041_R_d73763123e1083" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Small">41</a></sup></span> Additionally, multiple weak redox peaks, corresponding to the different processes of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction at various sites, were observed, following the extraction sequence of Na3→Na1→Na4 based on the different coordination numbers and bonding energies (Na3: 5-coordinated; Na1: 6-coordinated and Na4: 6-coordinated).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0018_R_d73763123e1089" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a></sup></span> With the increase of Ni-doping amount, the redox peak at Na3 position becomes more and more obvious, indicating that Ni doping effectively promotes the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics, which is consistent with the results of the following calculation. In addition, THoCS-0.6Ni exhibits the highest peak current and high electrochemical reactivity, which is conducive to rapid sodium storage. Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0003">3b</a> displays the charge and discharge curves of THoCS-xNi samples at 1 C. It can be observed that THoCS-0.6Ni releases a specific capacity of 117.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C, which is higher than that of THoCS (100.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), THoCS-0.3Ni (96.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and THoCS-0.9Ni (83.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Meanwhile, two voltage plateaus at approximately 3.2 and 2.8 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) of THoCS-0.6Ni with the smallest polarization are observed, coinciding with the redox peaks in the CV curve. The rate performances of four samples are performed as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0003">3c</a>. Among them, the THoCS-0.6Ni exhibits the highest capacities (120.3, 117.5, 102.9, 96.4, 91.6, 88.7, 87, and 86.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 15, 20, and 25 C) with exceptional fast-charge tolerance capability. Furthermore, the voltage plateau in discharge curves of THoCS-0.6Ni at 0.5–25 C (Figure S14) decreases more slowly with the increase of the current density compared with others, proving the excellent rate performance of THoCS-0.6Ni, and the appropriate content of Ni-doping effectively suppresses the rapid capacity decay. Even compared to previously reported works (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0003">3d</a>),<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0017_R_d73763123e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0023_R_d73763123e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0032_R_d73763123e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Eng. Sci.">32</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0035_R_d73763123e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Power Sources">35</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0042_R_d73763123e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Energy">42</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0043_R_d73763123e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Power Sources">43</a></sup></span> the THoCS-0.6Ni still exhibits a significant advantage at different current rates. The cyclic performance of four samples at 1 C in the voltage window of 1.5–4.2 V was detected as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0003">3e</a>. The capacity decayed in the first few cycles due to electrode activation, and electrolyte decomposition, but stabilized after the first 5 cycles. Compared to the capacity retentions of the other three samples (THoCS: 90.63 %, THoCS-0.3Ni: 90.60 %, and THoCS-0.9Ni: 91.04 %), the THoCS-0.6Ni sample has displayed the highest value of 94.16 % at 1 C after 100 cycles. Furthermore, compared with samples with different CA/PVP ratios, THoCS-0.6Ni (CA/PVP=8.4/1) exhibits the highest energy density due to its unique regular hollow core–shelled sphere with tiny-void space (Figure S15). The long-term cycling performance of the THoCS-0.6Ni electrode at an ultra-high rate of 20 C was also characterized as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0003">3g</a>, maintaining a reversible capacity of 60.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high retention rate of 72.96 % after 5000 cycles, demonstrating its excellent cyclic stability even under high current density conditions. In addition, the SEM images after cycling further prove the excellent structural stability of THoCS-0.6Ni (Figure S16). That cycle stability at such a high rate is exhibiting strong competitiveness to most of the previously reported literature (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0003">3f</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0023_R_d73763123e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0025_R_d73763123e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0032_R_d73763123e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Eng. Sci.">32</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0041_R_d73763123e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Small">41</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0043_R_d73763123e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Power Sources">43</a>-<a href="#anie202410590-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0047_R_d73763123e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">47</a></sup></span> This impressive electrochemical performance is attributed to the advantages of optimized doping-amount of Ni with larger cation radius and hollow core–shelled with tiny-void space ensuring the full carbon encapsulation to each THoCS nanoparticle and the interconnected carbon network for fast electron transmission, as well as plenty of tiny cavities for good electrolyte infiltration allowing high ionic diffusion.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410590-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/986f287f-5689-4a39-b4c4-355e1fd3969c/anie202410590-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/986f287f-5689-4a39-b4c4-355e1fd3969c/anie202410590-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f91871eb-d82e-48d7-a4ac-2b136d9dec5f/anie202410590-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/986f287f-5689-4a39-b4c4-355e1fd3969c/anie202410590-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410590-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410590" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance of THoCS-xNi samples. (a) CV curves; (b) The galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the tenth cycle at 1 C (1 C=129 mA g<sup>−1</sup>); (c) The rate performance; (d) Comparison of rate capability with literature; (e) Cycling performance at 1 C; (f) Comparison of cycle stability at 20 C with references; (g) The cycling performance of THoCS-0.6Ni at 20 C for 5000 cycles.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The low-temperature Na-storage performance, measured at −20 °C, was displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0004">4a</a>, comparing the different voltage profiles of THoCS and THoCS-0.6Ni electrodes at 1 C. Obviously, the overpotential gap between the charge and discharge curves of the THoCS-0.6Ni (0.62 V) is narrower than that of the THoCS electrode (0.95 V), confirming its faster reaction kinetics and lower electrode polarization at low temperatures. It also delivers a stable and high capacity of 90.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 100 cycles at 1 C, compared to THoCS (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0004">4b</a>). Interestingly, when returned to room temperature, the reversible capacity returns to 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with good cycling stability, verifying the good capacity maintaining and recovering capability at both low temperature and room temperature. The rate capability at −20 °C THoCS-0.6Ni electrode was examined, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0004">4c</a>, delivering high capacities ranging from 89.58 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> to 44.28 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C to 10 C and the corresponding voltage-capacity curves of THoCS-0.6Ni and THoCS at different current densities are presented in Figure S17. Moreover, even though both of them have witnessed voltage drop with the increasing current densities, the values of THoCS-0.6Ni are displaying small variations, even at the high current rate of 10 C, indicating a great practical application potential for fast-charge and low-temperature sodium storage (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0004">4d</a>). Meanwhile, the THoCS-0.6Ni electrode, pre-cycled at 1 C for 100 cycles for electrode stability, delivers a discharge capacity of 43.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C with a retention of 65.2 % after 2500 cycles at −20 °C, further illustrating the wide temperature tolerance, and surpassing the previously reports (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0004">4e</a> and Table S3). The reasons for the outstanding electrochemical performance of THoCS-0.6Ni with both fast-charge and stable low-temperature sodium-storage features are further by electroanalytical methods. Firstly, the diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> in all samples was investigated using the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). Among them, the diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) of THoCS-0.6Ni, ranging from 10<sup>−10</sup> to 10<sup>−8.5</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, is much higher than those of other samples, indicating that the appropriate Ni-doping significantly enhanced the cation diffusion rates of sodium (Figure S18). The electrochemical reaction behaviors were further investigated through cyclic voltammetry (CV) with different scan rates as displayed in Figure S19. According to the relation between peaks current and scan rate, the b values of THoCS, THoCS-0.3Ni, THoCS-0.6Ni, and THoCS-0.9Ni are calculated to be 0.6271, 0.6732, 0.7083, and 0.7758, respectively (Figure S20). The higher b value exhibits a greater capacitive contribution resulting from an increased electrochemical active area. Meanwhile, the specific capacitance contribution of THoCS and ThoCS-0.6Ni at different scan rates is calculated based on these results.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0048_R_d73763123e1201" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Energy Environ. Sci.">48</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0049_R_d73763123e1201" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">49</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure S21, the capacitive capacity contribution of THoCS-0.6Ni is higher than THoCS, which increases from 86.9 % to 93.5 % as the scan rate changes from 0.2 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The effect of Ni doping on Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics and the electrochemical resistance of electrodes are examined by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). In the EIS diagram (Figure S22a), THoCS-0.6Ni exhibits the smallest semicircle, related to the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), compared to THoCS, THoCS-0.3Ni, and THoCS-0.9Ni, indicating that the appropriate doping amount of Ni leads to lowest <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value. Moreover, the diagram of the relationship between Z′ and ω<sup>−1/2</sup> of THoCS-0.6Ni clearly shows the highest diffusion coefficient, coinciding with the results of GITT (Figure S22b).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410590-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c0a807ac-de79-427e-a97d-1b9d67fa880b/anie202410590-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c0a807ac-de79-427e-a97d-1b9d67fa880b/anie202410590-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ae2eb4c5-5e1e-4e07-8660-8d541fa298ff/anie202410590-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c0a807ac-de79-427e-a97d-1b9d67fa880b/anie202410590-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410590-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410590" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance of THoCS and THoCS-0.6Ni at low temperature. (a) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles, and (b) cycling performance at 1 C and −20 °C; (c) The rate performance at −20 °C; (d) Voltage drops of THoCS and THoCS-0.6Ni at different rates; (e) The cycling performance of THoCS-0.6Ni at 5 C and −20 °C; (f) The rate performances of THoCS-0.6Ni//HC full cell; (g) Ragone plots; (h) Comparison with other reported full cells in terms of capacity and average working voltage.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further explore the practical application possibility, the THoCS-0.6Ni electrode as cathode and hard carbon (HC) as anode were assembled (Figure S23a), marked as THoCS-0.6Ni//HC full cell. The charge and discharge curves of two samples in a half cell are illustrated in Figure S23b. According to the voltage plateau and specific capacity ratio, the cut-off voltage and mass ratio of full cells are confirmed. The charge and discharge curves of the full cell are presented in Figure S23c, displaying a long platform at 2.9 V and a short one at 2.7 V. The full cell has presented impressive rate capability, exhibiting high reversible capacities of 102.2, 89.3, 82, 74.1, and 65.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C, displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0004">4f</a>. A high reversible capacity of 54 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is still retained at 10 C, implying exceptional fast-charge capability (signifying fully charged in 6 minutes), and when the current density is returned to 0.2 C, a specific capacity of 104 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is recovered, illustrating excellent reaction reversibility. Furthermore, the THoCS-0.6Ni//HC full cell has also shown remarkable cyclability at 1 C in Figure S24, maintaining a high capacity retention ratio of 74.7 % after 200 cycles. Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0004">4g</a> shows a Ragone plot illustrating the energy density and power density of our THoCS-0.6Ni//HC full battery system at various current densities, demonstrating its outstanding performance in comparison with other reported full-cell systems.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0050_R_d73763123e1255" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Energy Storage Mater.">50</a>-<a href="#anie202410590-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0054_R_d73763123e1255" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Small">54</a></sup></span> Compared to the reported polyanion cathode materials,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0018_R_d73763123e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0041_R_d73763123e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Small">41</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0046_R_d73763123e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Mater. Chem. A">46</a>, <a href="#anie202410590-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0055_R_d73763123e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - RSC Adv.">55</a>-<a href="#anie202410590-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0060_R_d73763123e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Small Methods">60</a></sup></span> THoCS-0.6Ni//HC full cell has significant advantages at both energy densities and working potentials (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0004">4h</a>).</p>
         
         <p>To further investigate the impact of Ni doping on tuning the intrinsic conductivity of electrons and ions in THoCS, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were employed. Firstly, the crystal structures with different Ni doping contents, ranging from 0 to 0.9, were optimized as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">5a</a>–<a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">d</a>. Based on the relaxed structure configurations and charge density slices in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">5e</a>–<a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">h</a>, the charge density differences reflect the electron distributions with the bond length changes. Among them, the blue and red regions refer to the loss and taking electron clouds, respectively. It can be found that the charge density distribution around the P and O atoms has almost no change after Ni doping due to the strong covalent bonds in the [PO<sub>4</sub>] and [P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>] units. However, the doping of Ni with different amounts leads to the redistribution of electron cloud density around the Na and Fe atoms. Compared to the other three samples, the electron cloud density in THoCS-0.6Ni is uniformly enriched around Na and Fe atoms due to a larger cation radius of Ni and contributing electrons. The DOS pattern of THoCS-0.6Ni exhibits the smallest band gap (0.17 eV), which is significantly smaller than pure THoCS (2.76 eV) and THoCS-0.3Ni/-0.9Ni patterns as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">5i</a>, illustrating the enhanced the intrinsic electronic conductivity. Moreover, the PDOS patterns of THoCS-0.6Ni in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">5j</a>,<a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">k</a> further demonstrated that the conductivity is mainly contributed by the d orbital of iron-nickel elements. The other orbitals (s and p) and elements (O and P) have nearly no contribution to the electronic conductivity of the sample. Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barriers of four samples are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">5l</a>, the lattice volume of THoCS-xNi increases with the increase of doping Ni, thus the Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy gradually decreases as well as enlarged space for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, where the THoCS-0.9Ni has the smallest Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy (0.625 eV). Based on the diffusion energy barrier and path of Na<sup>+</sup>, the THoCS-0.6Ni exhibits the fastest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic. Therefore, during controlling the doping amount of Ni into the lattice of THoCS, the ion and electron diffusion rate can be optimized, thus facilitating the fast reversible electrochemical reaction, especially at high current density and low temperature. The corresponding migration paths in four samples were depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">5m</a>–<a href="#anie202410590-fig-0005">p</a> for vivid ionic migration behaviors. The aforementioned results further illustrate that with the increase in the doping content of Ni, the lattice and cell volume of THoCS are gradually expanding, creating more inner spaces for Na<sup>+</sup> migration and significantly improving the reaction kinetics.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410590-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a60f5d0d-cfa9-475c-a898-55d7e886fad5/anie202410590-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a60f5d0d-cfa9-475c-a898-55d7e886fad5/anie202410590-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e5452a4b-7fc3-49a9-9655-44e6c982b2b6/anie202410590-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a60f5d0d-cfa9-475c-a898-55d7e886fad5/anie202410590-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410590-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410590" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The theoretical calculation results of THoCS-xNi samples: (a–d) Optimized crystal structures; (e–h) The charge density differences of four samples with different doping amounts of Ni; (i) density of state (DOS) and (j) partial density of state (PDOS); (k) Electron contribution of O 2p, P 2p, Ni 3d and Fe 3d orbitals of THoCS-0.6Ni in PDOS; (l) Energy barriers and (m–p) the corresponding migration paths of Na<sup>+</sup> in four samples.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In order to investigate the reaction mechanism of THoCS and further analyze the advantages of THoCS-0.6Ni on phase changes, in situ XRD measurements were employed with a specially designed device using a piece of pure beryllium foil as a window for X-ray scanning, thin aluminum foil as the current collector for cathodes and sodium foil as the counter electrode. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0006">6a</a>, the (022) plane, centered at 32.4°, is gradually shifting to the left during the charging process, relating to the extraction of sodium cation from the cell structure of THoCS, leading to the slight lattice shrink, and the shrink reached to the end till it is fully charged. Then, during the discharging process, the peak at 32.8° is gradually returning the original position with the insertion of sodium cations, and the 2<sup>nd</sup> charge/discharge processes experience similar things to the first cycle, illustrating the reversible reaction mechanism of THoCS. The (222) and (421) diffraction peaks experienced peak disappearance at the end of charging and peak regeneration after fully discharged, and the same trends happened in the second cycle. Similar phenomena are observed in the THoCS-0.6Ni electrode but with a slight right-shift at the initial peak position at 32.2° of (022) due to the larger Ni radius doping in the Fe position (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0006">6b</a>). Unlike THoCS, there is no apparent hysteresis of peak vanishment and re-generation at the (222) and (421) planes, splitting into two peaks with higher peak intensity and corresponding to 3 Na<sup>+</sup> mobility (Na<sub>4</sub>FPP↔Na<sub>1</sub>FPP) per formula,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410590-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410590-bib-0018_R_d73763123e1364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a></sup></span> associating to the rapid ionic diffusion, better crystalline and fast phase changes during charging and discharging processes, illustrating the decreased electrode polarization and better reversible phase-change reaction mechanism. The lattice parameters of the compared samples, harvested during in situ XRD measurements, are calculated as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0006">6c</a> and <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0006">d</a>, illustrating their good reversibility during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. The a/b/c values of the two samples display similar trends with gradual increasing at charging and slowly decreasing during discharging and repeated well at the second cycle. Whereas, the cell parameter values of THoCS-0.6Ni are all slightly larger than that of THoCS, even though they have the same varying tendency, implying the well Ni-doping and the slightly enlarged cell volume. The lattice stress of two samples was further quantified under different charge–discharge states (Figure <a href="#anie202410590-fig-0006">6e</a>). During the charging process, the lattice stress of THoCS and THoCS-0.6Ni increases gradually with the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> due to the electrostatic repulsive force and lattice expansion and then returns to the initial position reversibly at the end of the discharging process in two cycles. However, compared to THoCS-0.6Ni, the lattice stress of THoCS is much higher, close to 0.35 G, implying the Ni-doping substantially modifies the crystal structure of THoCS with effectively alleviated internal lattice stress and improved structural stability.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410590-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/05de5887-a178-4ae5-b2a3-c12bfbd55cfb/anie202410590-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/05de5887-a178-4ae5-b2a3-c12bfbd55cfb/anie202410590-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3bb06a77-4cad-419c-9ce3-11fe1b5482e1/anie202410590-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/05de5887-a178-4ae5-b2a3-c12bfbd55cfb/anie202410590-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410590-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410590" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The phase transformation, lattice parameter, and stress variations of THoCS and THoCS-0.6Ni measured in the first 2 cycles: (a–b) The in situ XRD contour maps with enlarged parts at planes of (022), (222) and (421); (c–d) Calculations on the changes of lattice parameter, and (e) stress variations during the charging and discharging processes.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410590-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410590-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we reported for the first time that a scalable synthesis of hollow core–shelled THoCS-0.6Ni with tiny-void space via a one-step spray-drying combined with calcination process. The different coordination ability and thermal shrinkage rates of CA and PVP were skillfully utilized, as well as the precise adjustment of the molar ratio of CA to PVP, which led to the tuning of the secondary particle structure under specific conditions. The unique structure of THoCS-0.6Ni provides sufficient space for just alleviating the volume expansion and facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic. Furthermore, the incorporation of Ni doping enhances both intrinsic electron conductivity and ion diffusion kinetics. Therefore, the THoCS-0.6Ni can reach an excellent capacity of 86.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 25 C. And excellent low-temperature performance with a high discharge capacity of 43.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under −20 °C after 2500 cycles at 5 C. Wherein, the DFT calculations and in situ X-ray diffraction have determined the diffusion pathways and the reduced energy barrier, as well as the highly reversible phase-transition reaction mechanism of the THoCS-0.6Ni without apparent hysteresis (with 3 Na<sup>+</sup> mobility and reversible reactions between Na<sub>4</sub>FPP and Na<sub>1</sub>FPP), coupling simultaneously monitoring cell parameters and slight fluctuating strains. This work has illustrated the success of synergistic strategy through the design of hollow core–shelled with tiny-void space structure and heteroatomic-doping for NFPP electrodes, which can be extended to analogous cathode materials for reversible batteries with fast-charge and low-temperature storage features.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
